> method of programming and therefore while GUI = OO,  OO is not = GUI.

True.  But can you name one non GUI environment where OO has demonstrated its
value?  Has any mainframe environment moved to OO for its batch processes?  How
about for its non-GUI data entry?

Sometimes they will use Java for their Web processes - and use OO so they can
use Java (as opposed to using Java so they can use OO).

I am not aware of a mainframe environment which even invokes objects instead of
called programs within its normal processing.
```

Howard Brazee wrote:

> True.  But can you name one non GUI environment where OO has demonstrated its

The first OO Language was Simula 67, the number indicating the year that
the standard was formulated.  This was considerably in advance of GUIs. 
C++ was originally developed nearly 20 years ago, before the Mac even.

In many cases a new development in OO will have a GUI because it is a
'modern' system and not because it is OO.  That is, it doesn't have a
GUI because it is OO, it has one because it is new.

There are, in fact, many examples of OO programs and systems that are OO
and not GUI, server systems such as Zope.

Richard Plinston wrote:

> Howard Brazee wrote:
> >
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> C++ was originally developed nearly 20 years ago, before the Mac even.

And it was designed for a particular task, which it accomplished.  People looked at
the methodology developed and determined that it could be used for more general
purposes.  These purposes tend to be complex integrated purposes - such as GUI
environments.  I have expected to see them more in database type applications, but
its use in such has been primarily in code generators (as illustrated admirably in an
other thread - code generators do not necessarily produce code the way we would write
it).

The theory I have read doesn't talk about OO as being useful for extraordinary
environments - it talks about it being suitable for replacing current structured
methodologies in just about everything.

But in academic circles, theories like central planning where the elite can structure
the world to their ideas.  There seem to be more Big failures than Big successes with
this concept, whether implemented with collectivist farms, local zoning, or supply
link systems.  Sometimes it is necessary.  Sometimes we have to risk the Big
failures.  Sometimes the components have been tested enough and don't put the
valuable data at too much risk (your typical GUI).  But your typical mainframe shop
needs plenty of motivation to risk its data. (and jobs)

> In many cases a new development in OO will have a GUI because it is a
> 'modern' system and not because it is OO.  That is, it doesn't have a
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> and not GUI, server systems such as Zope.

Server systems are big, integrated, and complex - and buggy (I am unfamiliar with
Zope).  Sometimes we don't have any choice, we have to use what will get the job
done.


> > Has any mainframe environment moved to OO for its batch processes?  How
> > about for its non-GUI data entry?
>
> First you would need to get the batch programmers to stop arguing that
> 'we have been doing it _this_ way since creation'   ;-)

I would need to find programmers who are willing to risk their company's systems
and/or to get their bosses willing to risk their systems.  Isolated programs fail -
and the companies survive quite nicely while the programs get fixed.  When integrated
systems fail, the costs are much higher.  And they do fail.


> > Sometimes they will use Java for their Web processes - and use OO so they can
> > use Java (as opposed to using Java so they can use OO).
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> No, OO has only been around 30 years, give them time to catch up   ;-)

They learned structured code in 10 years.  The benefits were way too obvious and the
cost was relatively little.

How many mainframe shops are there?  Why don't we see ANY mainframe shops which have
converted their business needs programming to OO?  Is every single mainframe shop run
by idiots who can't see how much they are losing?  Are the thousands of shops all
waiting for someone else to be first because they altruistically feel it should be
their competitors' turn to gain a competitive advantage?

Or have some converted, and we don't know about the ones who have done so
successfully?
```

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2000-11-03T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3A033311.E0DA6E1F@brazee.net>`
- **References:** `<svh8j1g3rddt29@corp.supernews.com> <39fe6bb0.43967193@news.earthlink.net> <pYCL5.4246$M85.36336@news4.atl> <ha9uvs8bf05sv2ma40aikclrtsm22nnkpd@4ax.com> <b_eM5.7901$EW6.58160@news1.atl> <TniM5.17591$xJ4.846781@bgtnsc06-news.ops.worldnet.att.net> <L1AM5.9950$EW6.70265@news1.atl> <3a032a22.63417730@207.126.101.100>`

```
Thane Hubbell wrote:

> That said, OO COBOL is going to be a great boon to COBOL programmers.
> Until I "got it" about OO, I too was skeptical.  What I found was that
> OO as implemented in C++ was largely a failure, haveing too much
> platform specificity.  Java -- and hopefully OO COBOL -- do not suffer
> from that handicap.

It could be.  I expect it to be for CoBOL programers who work primarily on
PCs.  Any environment where you want tight integration OO can shine.

But tight integration has its costs as well as its benefits.  The hardest
part of my current programming is related a copy member, data dictionary
entry or called programs.  These shared elements make testing and user
acceptance much harder.  Isolation is a type of security.  When program A
breaks, program B still works.   I can upgrade a thousand programs one at
a time instead of all at once.  It may be more work, but only one program
fails at a time - and that has tremendous value.
```

<a id="t-548e2b397e"></a>
## Thread: UML and Cobol — 3 msgs

### UML and Cobol

- **From:** "Adriano Comai" <comai@tin.it>
- **Date:** 2000-11-01T08:11:43+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8toffg0v0r@enews2.newsguy.com>`

```
Sorry if this is an old topic for the group...

Has anybody experience in modeling a Cobol application (new or existent)
with UML (Unified Modeling Language)?
Which UML diagrams did you use?
Which pros and cons?
Could you suggest any reference to books, articles, websites about the
topic?

The UML is a modeling language standardized in 1997 by OMG (Object
Management Group -www.omg.org ). It has the ambition to be able to model
every software system, written in whatever language (not only object
oriented).

Thanks,
```

#### ↳ Re: UML and Cobol

- **From:** Ken Foskey <waratah@zip.com.au>
- **Date:** 2000-11-02T00:55:46+11:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3A0020E2.78FC6100@zip.com.au>`
- **References:** `<8toffg0v0r@enews2.newsguy.com>`

```
Adriano Comai wrote:

> Sorry if this is an old topic for the group...
>
> Has anybody experience in modeling a Cobol application (new or existent)
> with UML (Unified Modeling Language)?

Cobol does not integrate with Rose yet to my knowledge.   As a design tool
however I recommend it but it is very expensive.   A free diagrammer is
argo UML that I have used a couple of times.


>
> Which UML diagrams did you use?

Most,  I like the actor stuff.    I like the sequence diagrams they speak
volumes to the programmer.

>
> Which pros and cons?

Pros,  pictures talk a thousand words.   Cons,  lack of integration to
Cobol


>
> Could you suggest any reference to books, articles, websites about the
> topic?

Rational is the site of choice to start at.   For further discussion
comp.object would be the place to ask questions, just do not mention cobol.

>
>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>

It does do this,  OO or otherwise.    You just apply the diagrams slightly
differently.  I do not think it would be a great representation of a
non-OO system however.

Ken
```

Kjeld Paab�l Hansen <paabol@12mail.dk> wrote in message
news:nUoK5.1809$262.166612@news010.worldonline.dk...
> When I started as a COBOL programmer some 13 years ago on a IBM mainframe
> site, we still used JSP. A couple of years later we abandoned it
completely.
>
> The problem with JSP was that, although it forced some structure into your
> programming, it was a precompiled language which generated ugly COBOL
code,
> with almost unrecognizable paragraph names and lots of go tos. After
> precompilation an ordinary compilation generated object code which was
> link-edited into executables, but during debugging you could only relate
to
> the precompiled COBOL source code, while you had to make corrections in
the
> JSP source. The JSP precompiler could generate structure charts of your
> program, but given the limited graphics capabilities on output devices
> (character graphics!), these charts often spanned 3-4 times as many pages
as
> the printed code, making them impossible to work with.
>

dbf

#### ↳ Re: Help: locating Unisys flavored PC Compiler

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2000-11-06T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8u6uti$oh9$1@mail.pl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<20001101061320.14720.00000165@nso-ff.aol.com>`

```
Don't know of any such product, but in general I'd expect there should be
analogous mechanisms available, either in the file system or in the language
itself, for at least some of these on the target system.

The VALUE OF clause specifies "file attributes" in general, and two of the
ones you list describe the external name of the file (FAMILYNAME and
FILENAME; note that TITLE includes both parts of the file's full name).  The
third, the AREAS attribute, specifies the segmentation of the file on disks,
and this, too, is less a function of COBOL than of the file system
implementation being used.   If there are no other significant attributes
being used, these shouldn't be difficult substitutions.

As to DYNAMIC access to SEQUENTIAL files, none of the A Series COBOL
compilers (COBOL(68), COBOL74 or COBOL85) eem to support that, at least as a
documented feature.  RANDOM, yes.  DYNAMIC, no.    DYNAMIC is available only
with RELATIVE and INDEXED file organizations.

And in the case of RANDOM access to SEQUENTIAL files, there's a real
"gotcha" associated with that application approach:  It's always the user's
responsibility to know whether or not a just-read record occurring before
the end of the file was actually written deliberately into the file in the
first place rather than just leftover stuff from some other file that used
to be where the file now is!  RELATIVE has the advantage of getting
"notfound" conditions in the middle of the file, and I think that's a lot
safer overall than perpetuating the brute-force approach of the
RANDOM/SEQUENTIAL combination, which dates back at least to the earliest
days of Burroughs Large System COBOL(68) (and probably earlier!) -- long
before the ANSI-74 extensions that included ISAM were added to that
compiler -- and has been maintained for compatibility and convenience ever
since.  .Better to modernize the application a bit, I think.

    -Chuck Stevens

Sff5ky <sff5ky@aol.comxxx123> wrote in message
news:20001101061320.14720.00000165@nso-ff.aol.com...
> Does anyone know of a PC Compiler vendor that has a COBOL
> product that works with the Unisys extensions?
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

In article <8u6uti$oh9$1@mail.pl.unisys.com>, "Chuck Stevens"
<charles.stevens@unisys.com> writes:

>to be where the file now is!  RELATIVE has the advantage of getting
>"notfound" conditions in the middle of the file, and I think that's a lot
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Thanks for the reply.

Unfortunately, most of these programs are from maybe 30 years ago in design
and many years of maintenance.  Some progress has been made to shift from
COBOL68 to COBOL74, but coming any further forward could prove to require 
some major re-writes.  Using such 'Brute force' methods we are able to process
our files significantly faster than using standard access methods.  The ISAM
option
has been used in places where multiple keys are needed , but some cases end 
up reducing run-time efficiency.  
We process millions of records per night and have a VERY tight window
to complete all batch processing requirements before needing to have the
files available for the next day's procesing.

The PC processing option is just an exercise in offloading some reporting 
processes or preliminary file editing processes.
```

#### ↳ Re: locating Unisys flavored PC Compiler

- **From:** "Denny Brouse" <denden@prodigy.net>
- **Date:** 2000-11-07T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8ualpg$341q$1@newssvr06-en0.news.prodigy.com>`
- **References:** `<20001101061320.14720.00000165@nso-ff.aol.com>`

```
This may or may not help your specific situation, but assuming you still
have source code for some of these programs, we ended up creating smaller
extract files for PC reports from our V-series main processing programs,
then downloading these work files to a PC via Core Technology Bridge32
download software (Attachmate also makes such a product). WE then processed
the "work" file with Microfocus PC COBOL 3.5.  We changed any FD's from
VALUE OF FILENAME to VALUE OF ID, ignored the FAMILY NAME, etc. and borrowed
much of the actual processing code from the V-series.  We ran into some
snags with Random Access files, since UNISYS and Microfocus handle such
files differently.

However, since we converted to the NX-5600 Clearpath system (formerly the
A-Series), we can now map a network drive to the mainframe itself.  We
switched to Fujitsu PowerCobol 5.0 and we're converting some mainframe
programs into that language.  The neat thing is that we can read most fixed
length files directly from the "mapped" drive and NXServices handles the
EBCDIC to ASCII conversion for you. Thus no download software or FTP process
is needed.  Our approach is to just load the UNISYS source code into
Fujitsu, compile it, and fix the errors.  Obviously, any system "call"
routines have to be examined, but we are primarily just doing this process
for straight-forward reporting type programs that don't need system call
features.  Haven't tried any RANDOM files yet.  Suprisingly, we haven't had
to do as much work as I had originally thought we would.  And the PowerCobol
Windows interface makes it look as though you were writing in any other
Windows programming language.  Can't tell you how many people have asked if
the application was written in Visual Basic, C++, JAVA, etc. :~).

As a disclaimer, we never intended to use any PC COBOL product as a
"pre-compiler" for the UNISYS programs, which is why we moved from
MicroFocus to Fujitsu.  Since us UNISYS folks are "the other guys", we make
do with what we have, and you are correct in stating that most PC products
are slanted towards the IBM world.

Denny

##### ↳ ↳ Re: passing info

- **From:** "Michael Mattias" <michael.mattias@gte.net>
- **Date:** 2000-11-01T12:32:40+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<I3UL5.81$i_5.38456@dfiatx1-snr1.gtei.net>`
- **References:** `<laEL5.1386$fv1.160585@news1.primary.net> <PLLL5.28133$Q8.6375743@newsrump.sjc.telocity.net>`

```
Platform is immaterial: Addresses (pointers) in one Win/32 process are not
valid in another Win/32 process.

As suggested earlier by someone else, you need use the CreateFileMapping and
MapViewOfFile API calls to share data across address spaces. (Or one of the
other officially-sanctioned methods).

FOR REFERENCE....

Purchase a reprint of my article, "Data Sharing in 32-bit Windows"  which
appeared in the October, 1999 issue of "BASICally Speaking" magazine at the
IMS web Site at www.infoms.com. (Four bucks US; I don't receive any of
that).

While the code in the article is written in BASIC, the text covers the
concepts in a language-neutral manner.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: passing info

- **From:** "Gael Wilson" <gael.wilson@merant.com>
- **Date:** 2000-11-01T13:14:16
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8tp8ph$qpc$1@hyperion.mfltd.co.uk>`
- **References:** `<laEL5.1386$fv1.160585@news1.primary.net> <PLLL5.28133$Q8.6375743@newsrump.sjc.telocity.net> <I3UL5.81$i_5.38456@dfiatx1-snr1.gtei.net>`

```

"Michael Mattias" <michael.mattias@gte.net> wrote in message
news:I3UL5.81$i_5.38456@dfiatx1-snr1.gtei.net...
> Platform is immaterial: Addresses (pointers) in one Win/32 process are not
> valid in another Win/32 process.
>

That is true, however...

> As suggested earlier by someone else, you need use the CreateFileMapping
and
> MapViewOfFile API calls to share data across address spaces. (Or one of
the
> other officially-sanctioned methods).
>

...if Tom is using a Merant product, the CBL_GET_SHMEM_PTR and
CBL_PUT_SHMEM_PTR APIs can be used in conjunction with the ability to create
shared memory via the CBL_ALLOC_MEM call. On Win32 that provides  the
CreateFileMapping and MapViewOfFile functionality between COBOl run-units,
which are implemented as processes.

In the example given, process a) would allocate shared memory and and use
CBL_PUT_SHMEM_PTR to associate a name with the memory block. If process b)
was executed via CBL_EXEC_RUN_UNIT it could use CBL_GET_SHMEM_PTR to get the
address back and then use linkage to access that memory.

Those calls are supported on Win32 and UNIX products from Merant ie they are
not platform specific.
```

#### ↳ Re: FTP Upload

- **From:** "Cliff Peterson" <cliffpeterson@spamcop.net>
- **Date:** 2000-11-02T01:53:39+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<DO3M5.17235$xJ4.806559@bgtnsc06-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`
- **References:** `<t00h8eb4jleac9@corp.supernews.com>`

```
> Is there a way to move an entire PDS to a server splitting up each PDS
> member into individual files?  Please provide example code.

Convert your PDS to a flat file with IEBPTPCH, like this:
---------------------
//PUNCH  JOB stuff
//COPY1    EXEC PGM=IEBPTPCH
//SYSPRINT DD SYSOUT=*
//SYSUT1   DD DSN=PDS.INPUT,DISP=SHR
//SYSUT2   DD DSN=FLAT.OUTPUT,DISP=(NEW,CATLG),
//    ... you'll need RECFM=FBM,LRECL=81 ...
//SYSIN    DD *
      PUNCH   TYPORG=PO,MAXFLDS=999
      RECORD  FIELD=(80,1)
      LABELS  DATA=NO
/*
-------------------
I use Easytrieve to format it into IEBUPDTE input records and reformat into
FB 80 recs.

//STPU010  EXEC PGM=EZTPA00
//SYSPRINT  DD SYSOUT=*
//INFILE    DD DSN=&&TEMP1,DISP=SHR
//OUTFILE   DD DSN=GRPH.SEL.FLAT,
//       .. this one is FB 80...
//SYSIN     DD *
FILE INFILE
    INFILE-HEADER     2  11  A
INFILE-FILENAME  15   8  A
    INFILE-RECORD     2  80  A
FILE OUTFILE FB(0 0)
    OUTFILE-RECORD    1  80  A
WS-OUT              W 29  A
WS-OUT1   WS-OUT      21  A VALUE './       ADD    NAME='
WS-INFILE WS-OUT +21   8  A
JOB INPUT(INFILE)
    IF INFILE-HEADER  = 'MEMBER NAME'
        WS-INFILE     = INFILE-FILENAME
        INFILE-RECORD = WS-OUT
    END-IF
    PUT OUTFILE FROM INFILE-RECORD
/*
--------------------------
I do a lot of mass modification to JCL PDSs, so I like the output of this -
an entire PDS as a flat file.  I can edit with SPF using excludes, overlays,
everything.  It's faster for me than FileAid.  I use IEBUPDTE to make a PDS
out of it again.

To get to my PC, I download this flat file from the mainframe, then use this
Fujitsu COBOL program:
--------------------------
 IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.
  PROGRAM-ID. EXPLODE.
  AUTHOR. CLIFF PETERSON
          PETERSON TECHNOLOGY, INC.

 ENVIRONMENT DIVISION.

  CONFIGURATION SECTION.
  SPECIAL-NAMES.
     ENVIRONMENT-NAME  IS ENV-NAME
     ENVIRONMENT-VALUE IS ENV-VAL.

  INPUT-OUTPUT SECTION.
  FILE-CONTROL.
       SELECT IN-FILE
         ASSIGN TO INFILE
         ORGANIZATION IS LINE SEQUENTIAL
         FILE STATUS IS IN-STATUS.
       SELECT OUT-FILE
         ASSIGN TO OUTFILE
         ORGANIZATION IS LINE SEQUENTIAL
         FILE STATUS IS OUT-STATUS.

 DATA DIVISION.

  FILE SECTION.
  FD  IN-FILE.
  01  IN-RECORD.
      05  FILLER            PIC XX.
          88  IN-HEADER               VALUE "./".
      05  FILLER            PIC X(19).
      05  IN-FILE-NAME      PIC X(8).
      05  FILLER            PIC X(51).

  FD  OUT-FILE.
  01  OUT-RECORD.
      05  FILLER            PIC X(80).

  WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.
  01  IN-STATUS             PIC 99    VALUE ZERO.
      88  INFILE-EOF                  VALUE 10.
      88  INFILE-OK                   VALUE 00 10.
      88  INFILE-NOT-OK               VALUE 01 THRU 09
                                            11 THRU 99.
  01  OUT-STATUS            PIC 99    VALUE ZERO.
      88  OUTFILE-EOF                 VALUE 10.
      88  OUTFILE-OK                  VALUE 00.
      88  OUTFILE-NOT-OK              VALUE 01 THRU 99.
  01  FILE-NAME             PIC X(12).
  01  FILE-NAME-SUF         PIC X(4)  VALUE ".TXT".

 PROCEDURE DIVISION.

      PERFORM OPEN-INFILE.
      PERFORM OPEN-OUTFILE.

      PERFORM READ-INFILE.
      PERFORM UNTIL INFILE-EOF
          IF IN-HEADER
              PERFORM CHANGE-DEST
          ELSE
              PERFORM WRITE-OUTFILE
          END-IF
          PERFORM READ-INFILE
      END-PERFORM.

      PERFORM END-PROGRAM.

 CHANGE-DEST.
      CLOSE OUT-FILE.
      STRING IN-FILE-NAME
             FILE-NAME-SUF
             DELIMITED BY " "
             INTO FILE-NAME
      END-STRING.
      DISPLAY "OUTFILE" UPON ENV-NAME.
      DISPLAY FILE-NAME UPON ENV-VAL.
      OPEN OUTPUT OUT-FILE.

 OPEN-INFILE.
      OPEN INPUT  IN-FILE.
      IF INFILE-NOT-OK
          DISPLAY "ERROR OPENING INFILE " IN-STATUS
          PERFORM END-PROGRAM
      END-IF.

 READ-INFILE.
      READ IN-FILE.
      IF INFILE-NOT-OK
          DISPLAY "ERROR READING INFILE " IN-STATUS
          PERFORM END-PROGRAM
      END-IF.

 OPEN-OUTFILE.
      OPEN OUTPUT OUT-FILE.
      IF OUTFILE-NOT-OK
          DISPLAY "ERROR OPENING OUTFILE " OUT-STATUS
          PERFORM END-PROGRAM
      END-IF.

 WRITE-OUTFILE.
      WRITE OUT-RECORD FROM IN-RECORD.
      IF OUTFILE-NOT-OK
          DISPLAY "ERROR WRITING OUTFILE " OUT-STATUS
          PERFORM END-PROGRAM
      END-IF.

 END-PROGRAM.
      CLOSE IN-FILE
            OUT-FILE.
      EXIT PROGRAM.
```

#### ↳ Re: FTP Upload

- **From:** Ken Foskey <waratah@zip.com.au>
- **Date:** 2000-11-02T23:49:53+11:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3A0162F1.3C7FCB03@zip.com.au>`
- **References:** `<t00h8eb4jleac9@corp.supernews.com>`

```
hollanjo wrote:

> Is there a way to move an entire PDS to a server splitting up each PDS
> member into individual files?  Please provide example code.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> http://www.help.com/

ftp 1.2.3.4
userid
password
ascii
prompt off
cd 'my.pds'
mget *
bye


Some FTP's will allow you to add or remove an extension automatically.

Ken.
```

## Thread: Editing Fields — 5 msgs

### Editing Fields

- **From:** Cheryl McIlveen <cmmcilveen@home.com>
- **Date:** 2000-11-01T16:30:18+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3A004456.E59C5B65@home.com>`

```
I am new to cobol and working on an assignment dealing with editing
fields. Could someone please help me to solve this problem:

I am trying to get a minus sign at the end of my field using the S in
the PIC clause, but I also need to use the Z in the PIC clause as well
to get rid of the leading spaces, I have tried SZZ9 for a field length
of 3, but it seems not to like that syntax.

Any suggestions??
Cheryl McIlveen
```

#### ↳ Re: Editing Fields

- **From:** "Willem Clements" <willem@horizontes-informatica.com>
- **Date:** 2000-11-01T16:37:04+00:00

- **Message-ID:** `<jvyyrzubevmbagrfvasbezngvpnpbz.g3ddis0.pminews@news.wanadoo.es>`

```

>I am new to cobol and working on an assignment dealing with editing
>fields. Could someone please help me to solve this problem:
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Try PIC ZZ9-.

The S is not for editing purposes, but for an internal sign.

Hope this helps

Willem Clements
BrainBench MVP for COBOL II
http://www.brainbench.com
```

#### ↳ Re: Editing Fields

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2000-11-01T11:19:20-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3A005EA7.87C55FDC@brazee.net>`
- **References:** `<3A004456.E59C5B65@home.com>`

```


Cheryl McIlveen wrote:

> I am new to cobol and working on an assignment dealing with editing
> fields. Could someone please help me to solve this problem:
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> of 3, but it seems not to like that syntax.

The "S" is not a visible minus sign, it just says your number is a signed
numeric.  But Z says that your number isn't a numeric anymore - a number
gets moved to it and it gets translated to a display only field with those
characteristics.

A "-" displays minus sign, similarly to the way Z displays.  I don't know
whether or not you want PIC --9 or whether you want SIGN IS SEPARATE or
what.  I would look up these options in your text to determine which fits
your needs.
```

#### ↳ Re: Editing Fields

- **From:** Don Hill <dhill@sc.rr.com>
- **Date:** 2000-11-01T23:58:26+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3A00AD83.A27142A2@sc.rr.com>`
- **References:** `<3A004456.E59C5B65@home.com>`

```
all you need to do is to code the edited numeric field as
ZZZZZ9.99999-  and move your un-edited field to this.
Your field could have different integer and fraction part lengths
different
than this example.

Cheryl McIlveen wrote:

> I am new to cobol and working on an assignment dealing with editing
> fields. Could someone please help me to solve this problem:
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> Cheryl McIlveen
```

#### ↳ Re: Editing Fields

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc@bellsouth.net>
- **Date:** 2000-11-02T08:54:31-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<IefM5.7924$EW6.58278@news1.atl>`
- **References:** `<3A004456.E59C5B65@home.com>`

```
"Cheryl McIlveen" <cmmcilveen@home.com> wrote:
>
> I am new to cobol and working on an assignment dealing with editing
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> of 3, but it seems not to like that syntax.

As others have pointed out, the 'S' is used to indicate that a data
item is signed, not to show a visible sign.  There are several ways
you can create a visible sign for fields displayed or printed.  The
sign editing symbols are '+', '-', 'CR' and 'DB'.

The '+' editing character will display '+' for zero and positive
fields and '-' for negative fields.

The '-' editing character will display a space for zero and positive
fields and '-' for negative fields.

Either the '+' or '-' editing characters can be used in three ways:

1. Single character to the left of the data field:
    PIC  +999.999
    PIC  -999.999

2. Single character to the right of the data field:
    PIC  999.999+
    PIC  999.999-

3. As a 'floating zero suppression' editing character:
    PIC  +++9.999
    PIC  ---9.999

The 'CR' and 'DB' editing symbols will display two spaces for zero
and positive fields and 'CR' and 'DB' respectively for negative
fields.  They can only be used to the right of the data field, like
this:
    PIC  999.999CR.
    PIC  999.999DB.

If you desire a space between the rightmost digit and the symbol,
you can interpose the 'insert blank' symbol 'B':
    PIC  999.999BCR.
    PIC  999.999BDB.

You can use only one sign symbol in a picture (the series of zero
suppression sign symbols is counted as one symbol for this purpose).
```

---

## Thread: Quantum Cobol ante Portas? — 1 this month / 4 total

_Thread started [2000-10](2000-10.md#t-2244f59d2d)._

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Quantum Cobol ante Portas?

- **From:** G Moore <gvwmoore@spam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2000-11-01T15:11:06-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<vd8uvsovf0mp6m4bfsc3l2kei198no9u68@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<8tjgu6$d66$1@news.mch.sbs.de> <lavrvskjgdql4umotkfs8b8rt2sgbibmld@4ax.com> <8tm2s2$d0s$1@news.mch.sbs.de>`

```
>> skip the quantum searches, cpu cycles are cheap. it's putting the
>> database into RAM that costs money.

>No. CPU is not cheap. In science and engineering,
>you have a lot of applications where you could
>use much more computing power easily - not so,
>however, in most commercial applications.

i didn't really want to get into this debate, but it appears i must.
engineering rarely use binary searches. and while you *could* do a
search faster, the time getting stuff from disk if the db is not in
ram is *much* longer than a few comparisons.

you put your money into new search algorithms, and i'll put mine into
more ram and database engines which use it. we'll see which company
lasts longer.

>Apart from table searches, there is another class
>of quantum algorithms already discovered: it is the
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>encryted for all foreseeable future.

despite an encryptions compromisation, you'd still need to reverse
engineer the listening program to steal the corporate data. and of
course, you'd have to be able to decrypt the listening program's
algorithms and have the host not notice the time differential. then
you have the security solution of callbacks, where if a number thinks
it is compromised, it calls back and tests the recieving data,
possibly picking up the hacker or at least determining if the data
matches.

>That does not have immediate consequences for
>Cobol or any other language - but it has
>for some commercial applications.

for high security purposes, you simply hire a security guard. for low
security, you simply write a few custom algorithms and human checks
into the system. for instance, yesterday i put a large purchase on my
credit card, and they called me to verify.

reply to email gvwmoore@spam.ix.netcom.com 
remove the spam

points in agreement snipped

<a id="t-a9c1162f3a"></a>
## Thread: Questions about zoned and packed decimal datatypes — 17 msgs

### Questions about zoned and packed decimal datatypes

- **From:** "Jonathan Gennick" <jgennick@oreilly.com>
- **Date:** 2000-11-01T17:18:23-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** alt.cobol,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<t0592ff6qb0t60@corp.supernews.com>`

```
I'm trying to round up some rather arcane information about the
representation of packed and zoned decimal numbers. I used to program in
COBOL many years ago (on a Xerox Sigma VI mainframe), but it's been a long
time and I've forgotten some details that I now want to know. If you can
answer some or all of the questions below, I'd greatly appreciate it.

I recall that the term "packed-decimal" is usually hyphenated. Is that
correct? What about "zoned decimal"?

With a zoned decimal number, the sign is typically overpunched on the
rightmost digit. That digit should then show up as a letter or some other
odd character. How is that usually done? How would I see the value -12345
given the following declaration:

05 my_value  PIC S9(5).

If the platform matters, assume that I'm on an IBM mainframe. I just need a
relatively common example of how this is done.

Packed-decimal numbers have their sign (usually) in the right-most nibble. I
recall that X'C' was used for positive numbers. Thus 12345 would be
represented as X'12345C'. What is the typical value used for negative
numbers? I think a X'D', but I'm not sure.

On my system, admittedly from the stone-age of computing, we used COMP-3 to
declare packed decimal numbers. For example:

05 my_packed_decimal_value PIC S9(9)V99 COMP-3.

Is this still true today? I recently saw a FAQ that leads me to believe that
the following is more likely to be used:

05 my_packed_decimal_value PIC S9(9)V99 PACKED_DECIMAL.

Is that FAQ correct?

One last question. Was COBOL designed in the '50s or the '60s?

I will very much appreciate any help I can get on these questions. I'll
check back on this newsgroup from time-to-time, but it would help me out if
you also copied me by email on any replies.

Thank you.

Jonathan Gennick
Associate Editor
O'Reilly & Associates
jgennick@oreilly.com
```

memory, and after having purused the COBOL FAQ I've already had to "correct"
my memory in a couple of cases.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Questions about zoned and packed decimal datatypes

- **From:** "Jerry P" <jerryp@bisusa.com>
- **Date:** 2000-11-03T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** alt.cobol,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ZUIM5.974$h46.82960@nnrp2.sbc.net>`
- **References:** `<t0592ff6qb0t60@corp.supernews.com> <3A02B339.6B3489E9@bellatlantic.net> <t05iuttbb2el5c@corp.supernews.com>`

```

"Jonathan Gennick" <jgennick@oreilly.com> wrote in message
news:t05iuttbb2el5c@corp.supernews.com...
> Bill,
>
> I assure you that I would not attempt a book on COBOL. The
information is
> for a sidebar in a book about a tool that just happens to support a
couple
> of COBOL datatypes. Most readers won't be COBOL programmers, and
will have
> no idea what a packed-decimal field is. They might find a short
explanation
> to be interesting.

Be sure to include the REASON for the existence of packed decimal
storage: Early mainframes were the tools of the accounting department.
Accounting deals with money - base 10, not base 2. So many mainframes
started off, specifically, with hardware to manipulate base 10
numbers. For example, the machine instructions Add Packed, Subtract
Packed, etc.

At least that's the way I heard the story.

Imagine an accounting program for an IBM 1130 written in FORTRAN using
floating-point representations with its attendant loss of precision. I
still have nightmares about it.
```

I once wrote a verify program and assumed (silly me) that a float would
work for the small range of values.   After writing the program and
testing it I ran it on a large file and it was out $20.00  (very
approx).   I switched from float to double (very big floating point) and
my problem went away.   If it was not a throw away I would have totally
rewritten it with fixed point, as it was I proved my point charge the
customer to the time to prove it was their error.  I also gave them a
copy of the source code to prove my point.   I am wondering whether I did
a bad thing :-}

#### ↳ Re: Questions about zoned and packed decimal datatypes

- **From:** Daniel Jacot <daniel.jacot@winterthur.ch>
- **Date:** 2000-11-03T13:14:54+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** alt.cobol,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8tudoa$6ia$1@nnrp1.deja.com>`
- **References:** `<t0592ff6qb0t60@corp.supernews.com>`

```
In article <t0592ff6qb0t60@corp.supernews.com>,
  "Jonathan Gennick" <jgennick@oreilly.com> wrote:
> I'm trying to round up some rather arcane information about the
> representation of packed and zoned decimal numbers. I used to program
in
> COBOL many years ago (on a Xerox Sigma VI mainframe), but it's been a
long
> time and I've forgotten some details that I now want to know. If you
can
> answer some or all of the questions below, I'd greatly appreciate it.
>
> I recall that the term "packed-decimal" is usually hyphenated. Is that
> correct? What about "zoned decimal"?

Not zoned decimal, but rather DISPLAY.

>
> With a zoned decimal number, the sign is typically overpunched on the
> rightmost digit. That digit should then show up as a letter or some
other
> odd character. How is that usually done? How would I see the value
-12345
> given the following declaration:
>
> 05 my_value  PIC S9(5).
>
> If the platform matters, assume that I'm on an IBM mainframe. I just
need a
> relatively common example of how this is done.

Depends on the declaration. If you define my_value, you'll get a
compiler error ;-). If you define 05 my-value pic s9(5) sign is leading
separate, -12345 will be printed -12345.
OK, assuming you have 05 my-value pic s9(5), your field will contain
1234N. The sign-nibble is in the rightmost byte of the field, it will
contain 'D5'x in your case. A positive 5 would be 'F5'x. The character
representation of the negative zoned decimals are (assuming EBCDIC):

-1=J  -2=K  -3=L  -4=M  -5=N  -6=O  -7=P  -8=Q  -9=R  -0=}

>
> Packed-decimal numbers have their sign (usually) in the right-most
nibble. I
> recall that X'C' was used for positive numbers. Thus 12345 would be
> represented as X'12345C'. What is the typical value used for negative
> numbers? I think a X'D', but I'm not sure.

That's perfectly true. More precise: CAFE represent positive value, D
negative.

>
> On my system, admittedly from the stone-age of computing, we used
COMP-3 to
> declare packed decimal numbers. For example:
>
> 05 my_packed_decimal_value PIC S9(9)V99 COMP-3.
>
> Is this still true today? I recently saw a FAQ that leads me to
believe that
> the following is more likely to be used:
>
> 05 my_packed_decimal_value PIC S9(9)V99 PACKED_DECIMAL.
>
> Is that FAQ correct?

I'd guess that both are used. It's a question of style.

>
> One last question. Was COBOL designed in the '50s or the '60s?
>

The answer is: Since the '50s until today. FLOW-MATIC was invented in
1956, the first COBOL specification was 1959. The most current
specification is at draft level and will be released soon (OK, 'soon' in
comparison with the age of COBOL ;-). Search the web about 'Grace Murray
Hooper' for details.

> I will very much appreciate any help I can get on these questions.
I'll
> check back on this newsgroup from time-to-time, but it would help me
out if
> you also copied me by email on any replies.
>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Questions about zoned and packed decimal datatypes

- **From:** SkippyPB <swiegand@neo.rr.com.nospam>

```
On Fri, 03 Nov 2000 15:48:26 GMT, "Willem Clements"
<willem@horizontes-informatica.com> enlightened us:

>On Fri, 03 Nov 2000 13:14:54 GMT, Daniel Jacot wrote:
>
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
>
It would be x'C5' only if the field was involved in arithmatic or is
part of a numeric operation.  Otherwise it would x'F5'.  Consider the
following:

05  MY-VALUE    PIC S9(5).
05  MY-VALUE-X  REDEFINES  MY-VALUE
                           PIC X(5).
05  CHAR-FIVE   PIC X(5)  VALUE '00005'.
05  NUMER-FIVE PIC S9(5) VALUE +5.

Now:

MOVE '00005' TO MY-VALUE-X.

or

MOVE CHAR-FIVE TO MY-VALUE-X.

would result in MY-VALUE containing:

x'F0F0F0F0F5'.

However

MOVE 5 TO MY-VALUE

or

MOVE NUMER-FIVE TO MY-VALUE

would result in MY-VALUE containing

x'F0F0F0F0C5'.

Regards,

         (o o)

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Questions about zoned and packed decimal datatypes

- **From:** Daniel Jacot <daniel.jacot@winterthur.ch>
- **Date:** 2000-11-06T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** alt.cobol,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8u5rcn$pn2$1@nnrp1.deja.com>`
- **References:** `<t0592ff6qb0t60@corp.supernews.com> <8tudoa$6ia$1@nnrp1.deja.com> <jvyyrzubevmbagrfvasbezngvpnpbz.g3gxy00.pminews@news.wanadoo.es>`

```
In article
<jvyyrzubevmbagrfvasbezngvpnpbz.g3gxy00.pminews@news.wanadoo.es>,
  "Willem Clements" <willem@horizontes-informatica.com> wrote:
> On Fri, 03 Nov 2000 13:14:54 GMT, Daniel Jacot wrote:
>
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Oops, I was careless. I explained the negative sign and was wrong about
the positive. You are correct, the IBM mainframe COBOL compilers sets a
'C' for the positive sign. That's not a COBOL issue, but rather an
ASSEMBLER (or, more accurate a CPU and microcode) issue. After every
operation, original 'F' is changed into a 'C'. That's why we don't save
zoned decimal values in our databases, but either character data or
packed-decimal data.

So, +12345 would display as 1234E and -12345 would be 1234N. The hex
equivalents are in EBCDIC:

positive: ABCDEFGHI{   negative: JKLMNOPQR}

hex:      CCCCCCCCCC             DDDDDDDDDD
          1234567890             1234567890
```

positive unsigned numbers.  Our solution was to have CoBOL programs move

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Questions about zoned and packed decimal datatypes

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2000-11-03T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8tuvjq$uvp$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<t0592ff6qb0t60@corp.supernews.com> <20001103094257.11010.00000342@ng-df1.aol.com> <3A02D21A.5F166DAD@brazee.net>`

```
FYI,
  The revision (next COBOL Standard) introduces the underscore as a "valid"
character.  Yes, there are some implementations that support it today - and
some that don't.  The original question mentioned IBM mainframes when
talking about "characters" that a byte with a sign overpunch would "display"
as (which is HIGHLY significant because of EBCDIC/ASCII issues) - so saying
that underscore isn't valid is reasonable - as IBM mainframes do not
currently allow this in user-defined words.

Similarly, COMP-3 is not (never was - never will be) "portable".  Many
(most?) systems do accept it to be equivalent to USAGE PACKED-DECIMAL (which
was introduced in the '85 Standard) - but this is certainly NOT something
that should be used without/recommended without mentioning that it is
"compiler specific".

P.S.  I am positive that Steve knows all of this - but others reading this
thread (especially in "historical" files) may not.
```

## Thread: COPY REPLACING — 2 msgs

_Topics: [`Language features and syntax`](topics.md#syntax)_

### COPY REPLACING

- **From:** "Virgin Net News" <paul.moffat@virgin.net>
- **Date:** 2000-11-02T09:31:22
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<VvaM5.2694$1B5.53408@news2-win.server.ntlworld.com>`

```
I have a copybook used in the DECLARATION section for handling file errors.
I use replacing to set the name for statuses and other things.  I also want
to be able to display this file name.  I currrently have coded in the
copybook CPFLEERR

MOVE WS-STATUS-:FILENAME: TO WP-FILE-STATUS
MOVE ':FILENAME:' TO WP-FILE-ERROR

and then have defined in the program

COPY CPFLEERR REPLACING ==:FILENAME:== BY SVMPAR

However the compiled listing show the following

MOVE WS-STATUS-SVMPAR TO WP-FILE-STATUS
MOVE ':FILENAME:' TO WP-FILE-ERROR

The COPY REPLACING function does not appear to replace when the field to be
replaced is enclosed by quotes.

Any ideas other than setting up variables in a similar way to the
WS-STATUS-* variables for each file?
```

#### ↳ Re: COPY REPLACING

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2000-11-02T11:01:43-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8ts6k9$897$1@slb7.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<VvaM5.2694$1B5.53408@news2-win.server.ntlworld.com>`

```
Add (don't change) a line to your COPY statement, e.g.

 COPY CPFLEERR REPLACING ==:FILENAME:== BY SVMPAR
                                                      ==':FILENAME:'== BY
'SVMPAR'
     .
```

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe)_

_Thread started [2000-10](2000-10.md#t-84eb08b33d)._

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Read a PDS member from a CICS-COBOL program

- **From:** Liam Devlin <liamddd@optonline.net>
- **Date:** 2000-11-02T10:22:41+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3A014060.D73E51DB@optonline.net>`
- **References:** `<39FDACF6.365BD35F@generali.it> <aoutvs8bb1ib62bjqm885qa69v9gok3n2o@4ax.com>`

```
Volker Bandke wrote:
> 
> On Mon, 30 Oct 2000 18:16:38 +0100, Massimo Pettenï¿½ <Petteno.Massimo@generali.it> wrote:
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> >

I have no experience with it, but CICS supports REXX and writing such a
routine in REXX is a trivial matter (allocating a specific member, that
is (reading it, too, but that's a no-no)). The traditional or standard
way to read it would be as an extrapartition queue. Are the members very
large, or is this a high volume function?

Anyone out there have any experience with REXX & CICS?

LiamD
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Read a PDS member from a CICS-COBOL program

- **From:** Joseph Katnic <josephk@iinet.net.au>
- **Date:** 2000-11-02T21:37:34+08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<josephk-03D8F2.21373402112000@news.m.iinet.net.au>`
- **References:** `<39FDACF6.365BD35F@generali.it> <aoutvs8bb1ib62bjqm885qa69v9gok3n2o@4ax.com>`

```
Hi,

Probably the best way to do this in the lastest CICS is to use a 
DOCTEMPLATE.

You define a DOCTEMPLATE with the DDname and member. (see Resource 
Definition Online)

Then you code the EXEC CICS api thus:

EXEC CICS DOCUMENT CREATE DOCTOKEN(token) 
TEMPLATE(template_defined_above)

EXEC CICS DOCUMENT RETRIEVE DOCTOKEN(token) INTO(area) LENGTH(length) 
MAXLENGTH(max_length_of_area) DATAONLY

area will now contain the contents of the member defined by the 
DOCTEMPLATE.
```

#### ↳ Re: Animator for cobol

- **From:** "Paul Barnett" <paul.barnett@merant.com>
- **Date:** 2000-11-06T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8u69o9$a3a$1@hyperion.mfltd.co.uk>`
- **References:** `<8ts86s$brc$1@nslave3.tin.it>`

```
Hi Bernado.

Find the version  by typing

cob -V

If you are using COBOL 4.1 or earlier, you can only animate a .int file, if
you are using ServerExpress, then you can animate .gnt or executable files
also.

If you have a .int file try typing

anim source_program.int

Hope this helps.

```
Mr Hubbell is yet again correct:

See the GetFileVersionInfo API call and its related functions.

Note that this only works if YOU compile the version info into the EXE (or
DLL) as string resources (see below for the resource file entry). When done,
the info WILL show up when you click in Explorer and do the "properties"
thing (it's not magic, Thane) ; or you can load the resource data from
within the EXE/DLL and use it in your "about" dialog.

I don't know if compiling resources into the executable is possible with
your compiler. But hey, it works great with my BASIC-language stuff.
```

#### ↳ Re: Utility needed

- **From:** "Lucas, Todd" <Todd.Lucas@C-Cubed.net>

```
If you can read / understand Visual Basic I can send you a project to do
this.  It can return these properties for EXE, DLL, and OCX files.

If not then check out the GetFileVersionInfoSize, GetFileVersionInfo,
and VerQueryValue WIN32 API calls.  You should be abe to do it within FJ
Cobol.

<a id="t-c59c7a81cb"></a>
## Thread: Database Field Setup — 1 this month / 10 total

_Topics: [`Databases and SQL`](topics.md#databases)_

_Thread started [2000-10](2000-10.md#t-c59c7a81cb)._

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Database Field Setup

- **From:** cbass2112@my-deja.com
- **Date:** 2000-11-03T02:05:26+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8tt6h3$8vd$1@nnrp1.deja.com>`
- **References:** `<39F693F4.448E535E@labyrinth.net.au> <8ta4va$404$1@nnrp1.deja.com> <39F99375.454BE6E5@zip.com.au>`

```
In article <39F99375.454BE6E5@zip.com.au>,
  waratah@spamcop.net wrote:
> cbass2112@my-deja.com wrote:
>
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> > things.

-- snip --

> > If you don't want to break the multiple occurrence field(s) out
> > into a seperate table, for whatever reason, then do not use a
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> queries but it would make row retrieval and locking easier.

These are valid observations, to be sure. My position is simply this:
Don't Use A Different Technology For The Sake Of Using A Different
Technology.

MBS is redeveloping their systems onto a different compiler/platform,
which is enough of job as it is. There is no good reason to further
complicate things by also changing the underlying data storage
mechanism, especially when the new mechanism (RDBMS) doesn't directly
support the same things that the old one (VSAM/ISAM) supported.

Take things one step at a time.  Think about ramifications, pros, cons,
etc.  Think about the advantages offered by a RDBMS, and whether it's
more feasible to exploit them, or leave well enough alone.

Why use a RDBMS if it doesn't offer any real advantage?

I am just trying to discourage biting off more than one can chew, and
taking the big bite for the wrong reasons. There are few things I find
more discouraging than trying to hammer a technology into an
application for which it is ill-suited.

Curtis


Sent via Deja.com http://www.deja.com/
Before you buy.
```

---

```
On Fri, 3 Nov 2000 02:36:07, "Patrick" <ottopatrick@hotmail.com> 
wrote:

> sorry this is so elementary, but i don't have a manual that describes how a
> signed cobol field is formatted and i need to convert it to a decimal
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

How is the field defined?

If the field is comp this is usually a binary integer of some sort.

Others are comp-3 (packed with each digit a nibble low order nibble is
the sign) 
comp-1 which is floating point (I think)
and regular signed ASCII (this might be complicated by ASCII sign 
indicators or EBCDIC signs)

Some compilers (Microfocus) support comp-5 which is "native" binary.
```

That's scarey.  If your mainframe was an EBCDIC (IBM) machine and the field

assumptions which may not have worked.  Also the embedded sign may have

access to the source data on the mainframe?  Do you still have access to the

```
I have a 32-bit Windows DLL which will convert COBOL data to IEEE data (VB
compatible); it will also handle EBCDIC-ASCII conversions if requested. You
call the DLL twice: once, you give it the COBOL PICTURE and USAGE clauses
and it returns a bunch of data, which you pass to the routine a second time
along with the data, and the procedure returns converted data in an
IEEE-friendly format.

The kit includes BASIC (PowerBASIC PC/CC) source code to a data conversion
program which you may adapt to your VB application. (It also includes a two
copies of the same test file, one ASCII, one EBCDIC).

Write me for a kit. It's almost free: it will cost you the time to comment
on it.
```

```
Here's the kind of idiocy your CIO's are getting for the hefty sums they pay to
the Gartner Group:

"...Gartner has warned IS organizations that migrating their mainframe COBOL
developers to Internet and Java development would be a painful experience.
Here, we measure in dollars just how painful this process would be...." 

For the whole report, which informs CIO's that newly hired Java programmers
will start out 5 times more productive than your newly retrained COBOL
programmers, check out
http://gartner5.gartnerweb.com/public/static/hotc/00092702.html.

This week's Infoworld presents a nice rebuttal, pointing out some of the
study's more serious flaws.

Check it out at
http://www.infoworld.com/articles/op/xml/00/11/06/001106oplewis.xml.
```

If industry had kept sitting on its hands, as much of it was in 1996-
1997, it would have been bad.  Fortunately, even the very stupid did
get mobilized and got it fixed.  I was worried in those years because
I wasn't seeing people take it seriously enough.  By late 1998 it was
looking much better.  Except for the flakes predicting automobiles
wouldn't run and nuclear warheads would explode, many assessments were
fairly reasonable.  What couldn't be seen then was whether or not
people would get moving in time.  That's always a pretty tough call.

#### ↳ Re: Gartner Group: COBOL Programmers Aren't Worth Retraining

- **From:** Tim Josling <tej@melbpc.org.au>
- **Date:** 2000-11-05T12:16:05+11:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3A04B4D5.90C0C2F8@melbpc.org.au>`
- **References:** `<20001104121945.04179.00000458@ng-mk1.aol.com>`

```
My experience building transactional EJB applications is that one
ex CICS COBOL programmer is worth ten PC programmers. 

COBOL programmers are generally used to working in a disciplined
manner, and understand transactions and databases. In this
context learning OO was not too hard, compared to explaining
transactions etc to a VB programmer, or someone who has
'written'(well, watched the IDE write) a few GUI apps with Java. 

Gartner has a very patchy track record. They are great at
predicting the past, but not the future. They were about the last
people to 'get it' with linux.

Tim Josling

## Thread: embedded sql statements — 9 msgs

_Topics: [`Databases and SQL`](topics.md#databases)_

### embedded sql statements

- **From:** "David Duncan" <dduncan74@hotmail.com>
- **Date:** 2000-11-04T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<_nXM5.12843$Fs1.81529@news2.atl>`

```
I'm a student using a vax system.  I keep getting 'invalid host variable
:sql_patient' when  I declare a cursor.

Here's a part of my code.
         EXEC SQL
               DECLARE BILL CURSOR FOR
               SELECT PATIENT.T_PATIENT_NO,PATIENT.T_PATIENT_NAME,
               PATIENT.T_ADDRESS,PATIENT.T_CITY_STATE_ZIP,
               PATIENT.T_DATE_ADMITTED,PATIENT.T_EXP_DISCH_DATE,
               PATIENT.T_LOCATION,PATIENT.T_THIRD_PARTY
               FROM PATIENT WHERE        T_PATIENT_NO=:SQL_PATIENT_NO
          END-EXEC.

           EXEC SQL OPEN BILL  END-EXEC.
           EXEC SQL
                FETCH BILL INTO :SQL_PATIENT
           END-EXEC.

Please help. I have read the oracle manual and my database book, along with
my instructors sample code.
I modelled mine after the examples I had and it still doesn't work.
```

begin declare section
sql sql_patient_no  (what ever it is)
end declare section

This is more for information, than to be of assistance.  

I had the understanding that SQL variables (i.e. the items in the SELECT 
statement) could not have dashes.  Someplace I remember reading that SQL
has underscores and COBOL has dashes - but you could not mix the
two.  Host variables (used in the FETCH...INTO statement) can contain
dashes, not underscores, because these are COBOL reserved words.

Did I misunderstand something?

NOTE: This topic is relevant to me as we are beginning a unit on embedded
SQL using COBOL tomorrow night in my database class.  COBOL is the one
common language that all students have seen - so that is where we begin
our discussion of embedded SQL.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: embedded sql statements

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** thaneH@softwaresimple.com (Thane Hubbell)
- **Date:** 2000-11-07T00:01:39+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3a0745e6.160306246@207.126.101.100>`
- **References:** `<_nXM5.12843$Fs1.81529@news2.atl> <ZpzG4UNLyRNq-pn2-sdeCYO1EUtS3@tcpserver> <Gv1N5.13369$Fs1.87539@news2.atl> <3a057e37.43684660@207.126.101.100> <8u6fh6$ent$1@ins21.netins.net>`

```
GENERALLY - the column names in the database tables have underscores
(and MAY not have dashes for all I know).  Until the next COBOL
standard the UNDERSCORE is not a valid COBOL character so cannot be
used where COBOL variables are declared.

For my own use in my embedded SQL coding - I use dashes in my variable
names between my DECLARE and END-DECLARE statements  - and I make
those dashes correspond to where the database uses underscores.



On 6 Nov 2000 14:34:14 GMT, floydj@netins.net (Floyd Johnson) wrote:

What is the SQLCODE value that is being returned?

You should put a test after each exec sql like

01  display-sqlcode pic z(8)9.

if sqlcode not = zero
	move sqlcode to display-sqlcode
	display "cursor SQLCODE = " display-sqlcode
end-if

to see what the error is.
```

- **From:** Ken Foskey <waratah@zip.com.au>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3A050871.4A49F6F9@zip.com.au>`

> I modelled mine after the examples I had and it still doesn't work.

You might need to provide the table and varaible declaration to answer this
properly but....

a)  Are your variables declared with underscores or with hyphens.
sql-patient might work where sql_patient does not.

b)  as mentioned in another post

exec sql begin declare.

01   sql-patient.
    05 sql-patient-no    pic x(05)  (or whatever)
    .....

exec sql end declare.

Good luck
Ken

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: The Big Picture

- **From:** "Cliff Peterson" <cliffpeterson@spamcop.net>
- **Date:** 2000-11-05T06:18:57+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<lZ6N5.1245$mq1.105682@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`
- **References:** `<8serl4$8cd$1@nnrp1.deja.com> <29RG5.57352$bI6.1996851@news1.giganews.com> <8u1ubc$u6m$1@nnrp1.deja.com>`

```
<ylad2845@my-deja.com> wrote in message news:8u1ubc$u6m$1@nnrp1.deja.com...
> Please give me a definition of a "legacy system" and maybe a typical
> example.  I would appreciate it.

"Legacy" is a euphemism for old.  In normal DP use, it means anything in the
shop not written in the language or technique currently in vogue.  Often,
software salespeople use it to distinguish their product, as in "...and then
we'll interface (or convert) all of your legacy systems...".

I'm always amused to hear upper management say "our legacy applications"
instead of "our old crap", when that's what they really mean.

Cliff
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: The Big Picture

- **From:** "Joel C. Ewing" <jcewing@acm.org>

- **References:** `<8serl4$8cd$1@nnrp1.deja.com> <29RG5.57352$bI6.1996851@news1.giganews.com> <8u1ubc$u6m$1@nnrp1.deja.com> <lZ6N5.1245$mq1.105682@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`

```


Cliff Peterson wrote:
> <ylad2845@my-deja.com> wrote in message news:8u1ubc$u6m$1@nnrp1.deja.com...
> > Please give me a definition of a "legacy system" and maybe a typical
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> we'll interface (or convert) all of your legacy systems...".

This misuse of the word is indeed implied by those whose not-so-hidden
agenda is to promote a change to "their" development platform or "their"
vendor product.

> I'm always amused to hear upper management say "our legacy applications"
> instead of "our old crap", when that's what they really mean.

That's not what they mean.  The 1000's of application programs required
to run a major business and make it a success are not developed
overnight but over a period of years by many different teams of
programmers, who build on the work of prior teams.  In a very real
sense, any application that is a success and doing productive work
becomes a "legacy" application to those who follow.  Many PC weanies
don't understand that if you develop a workstation GUI application or a
web application for a company, the day after it is installed and doing
production work for the corporation, it is now a legacy system which
must be maintained.  

You have systems that are under active development, legacy systems doing
productive work and running the company, and archived systems (that used
to be legacy systems).
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: The Big Picture

- **From:** Foodman <foodman123@aol.com>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8u1350$a4n$1@nnrp1.deja.com>`

```
In article <39ebeda1.546069425@207.126.101.100>,
  thaneH@softwaresimple.com (Thane Hubbell) wrote:
> I'm about to be blunt.
>
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> >           GO TO K02A, E, K01, K09, K14, L L L L DEPENDING ON WS-
FKEY.
> >       K11.
> >           MOVE XFM60-SS-2-I TO XFM60-NEXT-FLD-ID.
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> unnecessary.

Hi Thane:

'Meaningless gibberish', eh?

If you had taken the trouble to read the 400 lines, you might
have understood the above, evidently, you didn't.

So, I'll spell it out for you again.

A. According to my methodology, 95% of all business application
   programs are manufactured from one of two skeletons.

B. One of these, the 'File-Maintenance' skeleton contains
   paragraphs K01 thru K20. These paragraphs are the ones
   which accept data from the user. Application-Technicians
   using the methodology would be TOTALLY FAMILIAR with
   the sort of code discussed here.

C. The application-technician changes the code in the 'K'
   paragraphs to reflect the application. Unused paragraphs
   are discarded.

D. My applications do not use the mouse. They are, therefore,
   fast, convenient and easy to use. As I mentioned, the Function
   keys are used to file a record, backspace to preceding field,
   jump around the screen, etc. eliminating the need for the mouse.

E. In order to accomodate this convenience, the RETURN-KEY is checked
   after each CONVERSE (in K99) and the program goes to the appropriate
   'K' paragraph or elsewhere.

F. In the 'K' paragraphs the first statement tells SP2
   which field to accept next. The second statement calls
   SP2, the third statement moves the panel-field to the
   working-storage field.

   If you think these statements are 'meaningless
   gibberish', complain to Bob Wolfe, not me. They are
   required by SP2 which you oughta know.

G. The PERFORM K99 statement was explained in the original post
   and is used to test the RETURN-KEY and convert it to values
   usable in the GO TO.

H. The GO TO goes to the appropriate paragraph depending on
   what the user typed. Just imagine how horrible these GO TOs
   would be if some idiot had changed K10 to K10-ACCEPT, etc.
   Only a ninnie would change the code in the original skeleton.
   (And they would be terminated shortly thereafter.)

I. All the application-technician does is change the MOVEs
   in the skeleton to reflect the particular field. The other
   three statements remain untouched.

J. As I said in the original post (or words to that effect)
   was that only a mindless dimwit would change K10, for
   example, to K10-ACCEPT-FIRST-PART-OF-SS-NO.

K. As I said in the original post, anyone working with this
   methodology would be thoroughly familiar with all of this
   and would have NO PROBLEM WHATSOEVER understanding it.

L. EVERY program of this type manufactured using the methodology
   would have identical code (except for data-names). EVERYONE
   would "get it".

M. If you were to take the trouble to read those 400 lines
   which you evidently ignored, you would see that this code
   IN THE CONTEXT OF THE METHODOLOGY makes SENSE.

N. This methodology is not for 'Real Programmers'. It is intended
   for the purpose of manufacturing business systems using ordinary
   people - quickly and economically - and it REALLY WORKS.

So, Thane, try reading the original again. If you still don't agree
provide code which meets with your approval, in the context of
MY methodology.

> Move Quantity-ordered to edited-quantity
> Compute total-price = quantity-ordered * unit-price
> Move total-price to display-price, edit-price

The above code is TOTALLY IRRELEVANT to the topic.

Thanks

Tony Dilworth

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: The Big Picture

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2000-11-04T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8u1n2n$koc$1@slb7.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<8serl4$8cd$1@nnrp1.deja.com> <39ebeda1.546069425@207.126.101.100> <8u1350$a4n$1@nnrp1.deja.com>`

```
I didn't comment "much" on the original note, but I did want to say
something now that this thread seems to be going "again". (Please excuse me,
if I "misrepresent" any of the original points or comments since.  This note
is INTENDED to only express my impression of what has been implied - without
claiming to "quote it" or state it exactly.

In the original note, it was stated that a couple (two?) skeletons could be
modified to handle a LARGE percentage of business applications (for this
person's programming business).  These followed specific rules which (when
understood/remembered) made each modified version (for each individual
client/application) relatively easy to understand, bug-free, and easy to
create and modify.

What may surprise some, is that I actually (mostly) agree with this as a
valid approach.  The reason that I do so, is that there were (and to some
extent still are) a NUMBER of "code generator" programs (such as Telon, APS,
Pacbase, etc) which do exactly this.  These tools provide a set of
"relatively easy to use" interactive tools that allow one to enter input
layouts, output layouts (files, databases, screens, etc) and relatively
straight-forward "business rules" to define how you get from one to the
other.  Furthermore, they provide "user exits points" where more complicated
code may be entered for customized situations.  Often (as this relates to
the original note) they use a "minimalist" approach to COBOL so the
"generated" COBOL code works on a variety of compilers (old and new).

Therefore, this certainly is a technique that works for MUCH of what the
original note said that it would.

NOW, however, for why I disagree with the "thrust" of the original note:

1) The first rule-of-thumb that the vendors of those tools tell you is NEVER
maintain the generated code; ALWAYS use the "tool interface" for
maintenance.  This lets you work at the "business logic" level and leaves
your code "maintainable" at level that OTHER programmers can work at.

2) For anyone who tries to "ignore" the first rule, the generated code is
UGLY.  It is completely consistent, so (like that from the original note)
once you learn its convention, you CAN understand it - but there really is
no reason to do so - and once you start "playing with it" at the COBOL
source code level, you make it so that it is NOT consistent for others to
read and understand (even if you "try" and use its rules). (Obviously, this
is ONLY an issue if you are trying to create/maintain code that others can
also "work on" - now or in the future.)

3) If the PRIMARY "thrust" of your business is creating code that does this
type of application, then (IMHO) you should use one of the tools that is
being maintained and enhanced to do exactly this.  Not only does it mean
that OTHER people do the maintenance and debugging of the actual "skeleton"
stuff (so you are "free" to do the money-making portions of customization),
it also means that there is a "base of programmers" that could maintain
these applications and that does not tie you to your customers or your
customers to you.  Now, this may appear to be a "bad business" decision if
you want customers who can't get anyone else to support their applications,
but I think "you" will find that more customers will purchase packages that
are not limited to one programmer for support - than otherwise.
Furthermore, (and this may be why I got out of "contract programming" as
quickly as I did), I believe that there is an "ethical" demand to provide
clients with something that does NOT tie them to me forever - while
delivering applications that will make them WANT to stay with me.

  ***

Bottom-Line:
 Yes, it is possible to do MUCH (certainly not all - and the original poster
never claimed it was all) applications via "hard to understand - the first
time a new programmer looks at it" - BUT consistent code.  Such code
(whether skeletons or created by tools) often can be "debugged" at the
skeleton/tool level - thereby producing (as was indicated in earlier notes)
almost 100% "bug free" code.
 However, if this is the type of application that your clients want, then I
would recommend using one of the vendor tools (code generators) for doing
this and NOT re-inventing the wheel for yourself.
 Finally, it is my impression (and I could be mistaken on this) that the use
of such code generators (for COBOL, PL/I *and* C) have all been losing
"popularity" since the mid-80's or so when they were extremely popular. (I
do NOT consider the various Visual "builder" systems to be part of this
group - but they certainly do "follow-on" the tradition). There have been a
number of studies on WHY this happened, but my general impression is that it
is due to the "limitation" of the generators themselves *and* the lack of
demand to enhance them to keep up with diverging application demands *and*
the lack of trained people to take over existing systems created with such
tools.
  I would be interested in "input" from any of the NG readers who have used
"code generator" software - both from those who still use it and those who
don't.
```

There is a case to be made that many programs in a
simple system are structured much the same.  For
example record maintenance and report printing.

This, however, is not an excuse for turning these
into meaningless collections of codes just because
'they will be understood because they are _all_
meaningless'.  Consistency may be an excellent
objective (hence you are aggressive towards a change
to 'K20'), but you started with meaningless code and
now you just have consistent meaningless code.

And the repitition of programs is not an excuse in
itself either.  I solved (most of) the file
mainenance issue by writing one large file maint
program that use a dictionary and a screen layout
text file so that just one program can maintain most
of the data files in a system (it calls a simple file
handling module to actually access the data file).

I have also written a report writer that can produce
any number of listings and reports from the files
(using the handler module) by following a simple
script and using the dictionary.

This means that I don't have to constantly reproduce
simple programs (such as yours) to build a new system
or sub-system but spend my time designing the
dictionaries, screen layouts and report scripts and
developing the unique programs that make this new
system actually work.

Setting up mechanisms to reduce keystrokes (such as
your 'methodology') just so that recopying programs
is faster, is poor use of resources

I have also written program generators where this
also creates the core code of a program from a
maintenance screen layout.  The text input also
includes things like validation paragraph names for
fields so that the generated code is extensible.
This approach also reduces programmer time in
producing standardised systems which are very easy
and quick to extend and change.

None of this code dissolves into meaningless codes
such as yours.

> N. This methodology is not for 'Real Programmers'.
It is intended
>    for the purpose of manufacturing business
systems using ordinary
>    people - quickly and economically - and it
REALLY WORKS.

From what I have read previously you have had very
little experience working with other programmers and
certainly not with 'ordinary people' type
programmers, though perhaps some who have never seen
other code may be more impressed with yours.

## Thread: Status number 39 on opening file — 3 msgs

### Status number 39 on opening file

- **From:** "Daniel Ratt���" <dratte@mediom.qc.ca>
- **Date:** 2000-11-04T01:26:14+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<SXNM5.3713$vr5.138222@wagner.videotron.net>`

```
Hello,

I have to read data from an input file organized sequentially.  I use a flag
to monitor the status of the file on opening, and when running the code with
the debugger I see that the status number is 39.  This is not among the
status numbers I was told about, and I wonder if someone could tell me what
it means.

Also, the records in this file are each supposed to have one 20-character
field, followed by 12 binary numeric fields for 4-digit integer values
(thus, 2 bytes each).  This makes records of 44 bytes each.  Now, when I
read this input file with a low-level text editor like Notepad or Textpad,
the records look like they are supposed to: 20 alphanumeric characters
followed by 24 characters of any nature, representing the bytes for the
binary integers.  However, when I look at this file with a binary editor,
these same records show that the binary-integer fields contain more than 24
chars in some cases, and this way the records are not of constant length.
What is this due to, and how can I read this data as 12 binary integers?  (I
am a student in a university Cobol course, and I have not yet seen how to
access files randomly with Cobol, nor am I allowed to for this project...).

Thank you!
```

#### ↳ Re: Status number 39 on opening file

- **From:** "Jerry P" <jerryp@bisusa.com>
- **Date:** 2000-11-04T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<tVTM5.2401$2Y1.215875@nnrp1.sbc.net>`
- **References:** `<SXNM5.3713$vr5.138222@wagner.videotron.net>`

```
Be careful, too, on the organization. If you're trying to read binary
data with a, for example, LINE SEQUENTIAL organization some binary
data may confuse the program into thinking that a carriage return has
been detected. Conversely, when you issue a read, the program may
slurp up the whole file  as one record, looking for a non-existent
carriage return.

On the other matter, trust the display from the binary editor. Again,
as above, text editors make the presumption that you are reading
non-binary text and will treat binary data, sometimes, as control
information (such as line-feed, or end-of-file).


"Daniel Ratt�" <dratte@mediom.qc.ca> wrote in message
news:SXNM5.3713$vr5.138222@wagner.videotron.net...
> Hello,
>

#### ↳ Re: Status number 39 on opening file

- **From:** "Terry Heinze" <terryheinze@prodigy.net>
- **Date:** 2000-11-04T00:43:56-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8u0b7p$bg0$1@newssvr04-int.news.prodigy.com>`
- **References:** `<SXNM5.3713$vr5.138222@wagner.videotron.net>`

```

39: The OPEN statement was unsuccessful because a conflict was detected
between the fixed file attributes and the attributes specified for that file
in the program.  These attributes include the organization of the file
(sequential, relative, or indexed), the prime record key, the alternate
record keys, the code set, the maximum record size, and record type (fixed
or variable).
    Most often, this happens when your program indicates a record size
different than that of the file's Data Control Block.
```

## Thread: CICS for Cobol — 10 this month / 14 total

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe)_

### CICS for Cobol

- **From:** guntergetting@my-deja.com
- **Date:** 2000-11-06T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8u65fp$p4$1@nnrp1.deja.com>`

```
Hello,

I'm trying to find out what CICS is exactly? And
what can I do with it in Cobol? And how to put it
together etc.
If anybody knows a good tutorial or some samples
about this please lemme know!

Greetings,
Gunter


Sent via Deja.com http://www.deja.com/
Before you buy.
```

#### ↳ Re: CICS for Cobol

- **From:** Joseph Katnic <josephk@iinet.net.au>
- **Date:** 2000-11-06T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<josephk-AB5581.22155406112000@news.m.iinet.net.au>`
- **References:** `<8u65fp$p4$1@nnrp1.deja.com>`

```
In article <8u65fp$p4$1@nnrp1.deja.com>, guntergetting@my-deja.com 
wrote:

> Hello,
> 
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> Before you buy.

OK,

CICS stands for Customer Information Control System.
It is a transaction system for IBM mainframes, but is also ported to 
some other platforms.

Users typically logon to CICS and run transactions. These transactions a 
4 character names that invoke programs. The programs may be written in 
COBOL, PL/1 and C/C++ or Java.

During the course of running the programs various CICS services are used 
to interact with the user (Terminal services), temporarily save 
information, read and write files, access and update DB2 or DL/1 
databases and intercommunicate with other CICS's via a form of remote 
procedure call or by shipping the above requests to the remote CICS.

To achieve this programs have "EXEC CICS" commands scattered through 
them. These commands are translated into the native language CALL 
statements and then the appropriate compiler is invoked to compile the 
resulting code.

Together with IMS (Information Management System), CICS runs the entire 
worlds financial transactions.

Hope that helps a bit.

You can download the PDF's of the latest CICS manuals from the URL
http://www.ibm.com/software/ts/cics/library/
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: CICS for Cobol

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2000-11-06T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3A06C084.2BFE026E@brazee.net>`
- **References:** `<8u65fp$p4$1@nnrp1.deja.com> <josephk-AB5581.22155406112000@news.m.iinet.net.au>`

> CICS stands for Customer Information Control System.

meaningless, but implies that IBM is responsive to customers.  I suppose it
does have an advantage - the very fact that it is meaningless means that we
didn't have any pre-conceived concepts on what it should be or do.  It is
just CICS.
```

> does have an advantage - the very fact that it is meaningless means that
we
> didn't have any pre-conceived concepts on what it should be or do.  It is
> just CICS.
>

If my memory serves me correctly, CICS came from FASTER which was
developed in the field by an IBM'er by the name of Gentry (if my
spelling is correct).  As I recall, he was doing work for Alameda
County in CA.  Later, IBM revamped it so that it became CICS. 
While I was doing work at Memphis TN (for the courts and PD),
OS/FASTER-MT was running under/inside of CICS (V1.1.1 and then
V1.3 as I recall).  CICS has now been upgraded so that it is now
called CICS/TS or TS/CICS or some such.  In order to make CICS
multi-task dispatchable, it appears to use the same dispatching
scheme as WYLBUR 8.1/9.0.  This means that CICS is actually a
program that goes back to circa 1969.  But, that's if my memory
serves me correctly, and I've lost a few cores over the years
(and a lot of hairs to go with 'em) and the ECC doesn't work
quite right.
